**Seminal work:** "Recent advances in the application of predictive coding and active inference models within clinical neuroscience" (2021, *Psychiatry and Clinical Neurosciences*); Trempler et al., "Ignore the glitch but mind the switch" (2023, *J Psychiatric Research*) — direct evidence that methylphenidate improves both flexibility and stability of prediction in ADHD.

**Q1 — What is executive function in the predictive processing framework?** Not a module but an emergent property. "Attention" is precision optimization. "Inhibition" is suppressing sensory-driven prediction errors in favor of top-down predictions. ADHD involves a systematic bias toward bottom-up processing — not a deficit per se but a different processing mode.

**Q2 — What happens to precision-weighting upon discontinuation?** Baseline precision imbalances re-emerge. Underconfident priors return, distractibility increases. The medication was tuning a computational parameter, not creating structural change. However, learning that occurred under optimized precision conditions may partially persist.

**Q3 — Can computational phenotyping guide personalized treatment?** Active inference models could identify individuals with primarily precision-weighting deficits (who benefit most from pharmacological dopamine optimization) versus those with generative-model deficits (who benefit more from cognitive-behavioral interventions restructuring internal models).

---

### Part IV: Synthesis

#### Five cross-cutting themes that should inform recovery program design

**Theme 1: The "double scaffold" problem.** Multiple experts converge on a finding with immediate startup implications: adults who discontinue amphetamines after years of use face not merely a return to baseline executive function, but a double loss — the pharmacological scaffold AND the compensatory skills they never developed because medication masked the need. Solanto calls this the treatment gap; Scheres frames it as motivational underdevelopment; Barkley calls it the chronic performance deficit that medication only temporarily bridges. **Any recovery program must build the second scaffold — metacognitive skills, environmental supports, motivational alignment — before removing the first.**

**Theme 2: The circadian-metabolic axis is a neglected recovery lever.** Kooij's circadian research and Rucklidge's nutritional work reveal two biological systems that stimulants actively damage while treating ADHD symptoms. Amphetamines delay circadian rhythms in a population already phase-delayed by ~90 minutes, and suppress appetite in brains that may already be nutritionally compromised. Recovery programs that fail to address circadian re-alignment (morning light, evening melatonin, fixed wake times) and nutritional rehabilitation (broad-spectrum micronutrients, gut health) are missing the most immediately actionable biological interventions. These are also the interventions with the fastest evidence-to-implementation pipeline.

**Theme 3: Environment design is as valid an intervention as pharmacology.** The philosophical and ecological psychology experts — de Haan, Levy, Rietveld, Chemero — converge powerfully on this point, which the applied researchers (Maeir, Solanto) confirm empirically. From the Skilled Intentionality Framework, executive dysfunction is dis-attunement to the affordance landscape; fix the landscape and you change the functional outcome without touching the brain. Levy's extended mind thesis destigmatizes dependence on external supports. **A "personal operating system" for ADHD is not a workaround — it is cognition itself, externalized.** Recovery programs should dedicate as much design effort to structuring the user's physical, digital, and social environment as to any internal capacity-building.

**Theme 4: Exercise deserves protocol-level integration, not lifestyle-level suggestion.** The convergence of Ratey's neurochemical narrative, Hoza's empirical rigor, and the meta-meta-analytic effect size of **d = 0.93** makes the case incontrovertible: cognitively engaging physical exercise is the single most effective non-pharmacological intervention for executive function in ADHD. It operates through the same dopaminergic and noradrenergic pathways as stimulant medication, with the addition of BDNF-mediated neuroplasticity that drugs do not provide. Ratey's insight that executive function is fundamentally embodied — that the brain's attention system is a movement-coordination system repurposed for abstract thought — suggests that movement-based interventions may be more neurally aligned than purely cognitive training.

**Theme 5: The identity question is real and must be addressed therapeutically.** De Haan's "self-illness ambiguity," Scheres' "authentic inner compass," and Levy's "enhancing authenticity" argument all converge on a psychological experience that many ADHD adults report but that most clinical programs ignore: *Who am I without my medication?* This is not merely philosophical — it is a clinical barrier to successful discontinuation. Adults who have been medicated for years may have constructed their professional identity, relationships, and self-concept around their medicated cognitive capacities. Recovery programs need explicit identity-work components — phenomenological self-exploration, values clarification, narrative reconstruction — alongside the cognitive and biological interventions.

#### Seven open questions that represent research and product opportunities

1. **No prospective adult discontinuation study exists.** The single largest evidence gap. Whoever generates longitudinal data on executive function trajectories after amphetamine discontinuation in adults will own the evidence base.

2. **Do learned computational strategies survive discontinuation?** Frank's models predict that corticostriatal gating habits may persist; Hauser's framework suggests computational phenotyping could predict who retains what. Neither has been tested clinically.

3. **What is the optimal sequencing of multimodal interventions during tapering?** Should metacognitive training, exercise protocols, chronotherapy, and nutritional supplementation begin weeks before tapering? Months? There is no evidence base for sequencing.

4. **Does the DAT upregulation Rubia warns about actually produce worse-than-baseline function?** PET studies of striatal DAT density before and after chronic stimulant use in ADHD adults would answer this critical question.

5. **Can mindfulness plus exercise produce neuroplastic changes equivalent to continued medication?** Both independently target DMN-executive network dynamics through distinct mechanisms. Their combination has never been tested against continued stimulant treatment.

6. **Do epigenetic marks predict discontinuation trajectories?** Cecil's framework suggests methylation profiles could identify individuals with more versus less biologically entrenched ADHD phenotypes.

7. **How should digital tools scaffold the transition?** Levy's extended mind thesis suggests that well-designed digital tools are literally part of the user's cognitive system. The product design question is: what digital affordances best replace the precision-weighting function that stimulants provide?

#### What this means for building an ADHD management startup

The topology of expertise mapped in this report points toward a recovery program architecture with five integrated layers, each informed by specific expert frames:

- **Layer 1 — Biological foundation:** Circadian re-alignment (Kooij), nutritional rehabilitation (Rucklidge), exercise protocols (Ratey, Hoza). These are the fastest to implement and address the biological systems stimulants actively damage. Begin before medication tapering.

- **Layer 2 — Metacognitive skill building:** Structured executive function training (Solanto, Maeir) delivered during periods of continued or partial pharmacological support, so skills are built while cognitive resources are available. Brown's six-cluster model provides the assessment framework; Barkley's point-of-performance concept guides when and where to deploy skills.

- **Layer 3 — Environment and scaffold design:** Affordance landscape engineering (Rietveld), extended cognitive system design (Levy), occupational environment modification (Maeir). Physical spaces, digital tools, and social structures designed to externalize executive demands and amplify goal-relevant affordances.

- **Layer 4 — Motivational and identity work:** SDT-informed autonomy support (Scheres), contemplative self-awareness (Zylowska), phenomenological identity exploration (de Haan). Reconnecting with intrinsic motivation and reconstructing a coherent sense of self that does not depend on pharmacological cognitive state.

- **Layer 5 — Ongoing monitoring and personalization:** Computational phenotyping (Frank, Hauser, Smith) to identify individual profiles of gain impairment, gating capacity, and precision-weighting. Network connectivity biomarkers (Castellanos) to predict discontinuation trajectories. Surman's treatment gap metrics to assess whether functional EF, not just symptom scores, is being maintained.

The experts profiled here do not merely represent different opinions. They represent different **levels of the same system** — from molecular synapses (Arnsten) through computational algorithms (Frank) through brain networks (Castellanos) through agent-environment dynamics (Rietveld) through lived experience (de Haan) through social context (Scheres). A recovery program that operates at all these levels simultaneously, sequenced intelligently and personalized computationally, would represent a genuine frontier advance in ADHD care — one that no single expert's frame can achieve alone, but that their collective intelligence makes possible.

---

## Amphetamine Addiction Recovery Through the Lens of Research-Grade Cognitive Science and Adjacent Disciplines

A Topology of Semantic Frames, Leading Figures, and Seminal Works Across Neuroscience, Computational Psychiatry, Clinical Psychology, Behavioral Economics, and Recovery Science

Compiled March 2026

### TABLE OF CONTENTS

I. State of the Frontier: Overview

II. Topology of Semantic Frames

III. Expert Profiles (22 Figures)

#### Cluster A: Neurobiological Mechanism Frames

#### Cluster B: Learning, Habit & Computational Frames

#### Cluster C: Clinical Intervention & Recovery Frames

#### Cluster D: Psychosocial, Ecological & Identity Frames

IV. Gaps, Tensions & Future Directions

### I. STATE OF THE FRONTIER

Amphetamine and methamphetamine use disorders occupy a uniquely challenging position in addiction science. Unlike opioid use disorder, for which multiple FDA-approved pharmacotherapies exist, stimulant use disorder has no approved medication as of early 2026. The most promising pharmacological result to date—a combination of injectable naltrexone and oral bupropion—achieved a response rate of only 16.5% in a Phase III trial (Trivedi et al., 2021, NEJM). This therapeutic vacuum has forced the field to draw on a remarkably broad coalition of research traditions, from molecular neuropharmacology to contemplative neuroscience, from computational reinforcement learning to community-based recovery capital models.

The frontier is defined by several convergent insights. First, amphetamines produce profound and persistent neuroadaptations in the dopaminergic system—particularly dopamine transporter downregulation in the striatum—that show partial but meaningful recovery with protracted abstinence (Volkow et al., 2001). Second, the subjective experience of addiction is increasingly understood not as a monolithic "hijacked brain" but as a dynamic interplay between sensitized incentive motivation ("wanting" without proportionate "liking"), allostatic dysregulation of hedonic set points, the progressive consolidation of drug-seeking habits, and impaired prefrontal executive control. Third, computational psychiatry is beginning to formalize these processes as aberrant reinforcement learning algorithms, opening the door to precision diagnostics and individually-tailored interventions. Fourth, the most effective behavioral intervention for stimulant addiction—contingency management—works precisely by restoring the reward signal for non-drug reinforcers, yet remains drastically underdeployed due to policy and economic barriers.

The landscape is further enriched by emerging paradigms: mindfulness-based relapse prevention, which targets the craving-behavior link at its operant root; trauma-informed models linking adverse childhood experiences to dopaminergic vulnerability; recovery capital and identity-transformation frameworks from sociology; neuromodulation approaches including transcranial magnetic stimulation and deep brain stimulation; and the nascent exploration of psychedelic-assisted therapy for stimulant disorders. Each of these represents a distinct semantic frame—a way of conceptualizing what addiction is, what recovery means, and where intervention should target. The topology of these frames is the subject of this report.

### II. TOPOLOGY OF SEMANTIC FRAMES

The 22 figures profiled in this report can be organized into four major clusters, each containing multiple complementary but distinct semantic frames. These clusters are not hermetically sealed—many researchers operate at their intersections—but they represent meaningfully different ontological commitments about the nature of addiction and the locus of recovery.

#### Cluster A: Neurobiological Mechanism Frames

These frames locate addiction’s essence in specific neural circuit dysfunctions. The Incentive Sensitization frame (Berridge & Robinson) holds that repeated amphetamine exposure produces lasting hypersensitivity in mesolimbic dopamine systems, amplifying "wanting" for drugs without increasing "liking." The Allostatic/Opponent-Process frame (Koob & Le Moal) emphasizes the progressive downregulation of reward circuits and recruitment of brain stress systems (CRF, dynorphin), creating a negative emotional state that drives compulsive use through negative reinforcement. The Dopaminergic Neuroimaging frame (Volkow) integrates PET evidence showing how stimulant-induced dopamine system changes impair frontal cortical circuits for self-control. The Synaptic Plasticity frame (Bhatt, Bhatt, Bhatt) examines the glutamatergic and dopaminergic molecular mechanisms through which amphetamines rewrite synaptic architecture. The Impaired Response Inhibition and Salience Attribution (iRISA) frame (Goldstein & Volkow) models addiction as the conjunction of hyperactive salience circuits and hypoactive inhibitory control.

#### Cluster B: Learning, Habit & Computational Frames

These frames conceptualize addiction as a disorder of learning systems. The Habit/Compulsion Transition frame (Everitt & Robbins) models addiction as a shift from goal-directed to habitual drug seeking, underpinned by a ventral-to-dorsal striatal progression. The Computational Reinforcement Learning frame (Redish; Daw; Huys) formalizes addiction as aberrant prediction error signaling and a pathological shift from model-based to model-free behavioral control. The Temporal Discounting/Behavioral Economics frame (Bickel; MacKillop) treats addiction as an extreme form of impulsive choice, where the steep temporal discounting of future rewards makes immediate drug reinforcement dominate decision-making. The Ersche Endophenotype frame identifies pre-existing neurocognitive vulnerabilities—impulsivity, compulsivity, executive dysfunction—as endophenotypes that precede and predict stimulant addiction.

#### Cluster C: Clinical Intervention & Recovery Frames

These frames are organized around specific treatment modalities. The Contingency Management frame (Higgins; Petry) applies operant conditioning principles to restore competing non-drug reinforcement, representing the strongest evidence base for stimulant addiction treatment. The Mindfulness-Based Relapse Prevention frame (Brewer; Bowen) targets the craving-behavior link by training meta-cognitive awareness of affective states, decoupling urge from action. The Cognitive Behavioral/Relapse Prevention frame (Carroll; Marlatt) emphasizes coping skill acquisition, cognitive restructuring, and functional analysis of use episodes. The Motivational Interviewing/Stages of Change frame (Miller & Rollnick; Prochaska & DiClemente) treats motivation as the central therapeutic variable, working with ambivalence rather than

against it. The Pharmacological Rescue frame (Trivedi; Ling) pursues pharmacological interventions to normalize dopaminergic dysfunction during recovery. The Neuromodulation frame (Hanlon; Ekhtiari) uses TMS and other brain stimulation technologies to directly modulate craving and executive control circuits.

#### Cluster D: Psychosocial, Ecological & Identity Frames

These frames locate recovery in social context and self-concept. The Recovery Capital frame (White; Cloud & Granfield) defines recovery as the accumulation of internal and external resources—social, physical, human, and cultural capital—rather than merely the cessation of use. The Self-Determination Theory frame (Ryan & Deci; applied by Zuroff and others) holds that sustainable recovery requires satisfaction of the basic psychological needs for autonomy, competence, and relatedness. The Trauma-Informed/ACE frame (Felitti; Maté) reframes addiction as a response to developmental trauma, requiring healing of the wound rather than management of the symptom. The Social Identity Model of Recovery (SIMOR) frame (Best; Beckwith) treats recovery as a process of identity transition, where the individual’s shift from an "addict" to a "person in recovery" identity is mediated by social group membership.

Key Tensions: The most productive tensions in the field run between Cluster A (mechanism) and Cluster D (context)—is addiction fundamentally a brain state or a social condition? Between Cluster B (learning/habit) and the Incentive Sensitization frame—is compulsive use habitual or motivationally driven? And between the Contingency Management frame (external reinforcement) and the Self-Determination frame (intrinsic motivation)—does extrinsic reward scaffolding undermine or enable autonomous recovery?

### III. EXPERT PROFILES

#### Cluster A: NEUROBIOLOGICAL MECHANISM FRAMES

1. Kent C. Berridge

University of Michigan, Department of Psychology | Behavioral Neuroscience / Affective Neuroscience

Semantic Frame: Incentive Sensitization — 'Wanting' vs. 'Liking'

Seminal Work: Robinson, T.E. & Berridge, K.C. (1993). 'The neural basis of drug craving: An incentive-sensitization theory of addiction.' Brain Research Reviews, 18(3), 247–291. Updated in Annual Review of Psychology (2025).

Synthesis: Berridge and Robinson proposed that addictive drugs produce lasting sensitization of mesolimbic dopamine systems that mediate incentive salience ('wanting'), a motivational process dissociable from hedonic impact ('liking'). Repeated amphetamine exposure hyper-sensitizes these circuits, such that drug cues trigger compulsive 'wanting' even as the subjective pleasure of the drug diminishes. The theory explains the paradox at the heart of stimulant addiction: people desperately pursue a substance that no longer delivers proportionate pleasure. Crucially, sensitization persists for months or years after drug cessation, mediated by structural changes in nucleus accumbens and prefrontal cortical neurons.

Q1: What is the fundamental computational function of dopamine in the reward circuit, and how does this differ from the folk-psychological assumption?

Dopamine's primary role is not to generate pleasure but to attribute incentive salience—to transform a neutral perceptual representation of a stimulus into something motivationally 'wanted.' This 'wanting' is a specific psychological process that can be measured independently from hedonic 'liking.' The folk equation of dopamine with pleasure is empirically wrong: dopamine-depleted animals still show normal hedonic reactions to sweet tastes, even though they lose all motivation to pursue rewards.

Q2: How does the sensitization mechanism explain the chronicity and relapse vulnerability of amphetamine addiction specifically?

Amphetamines are among the most potent sensitizers of mesolimbic systems. Repeated intermittent exposure produces neural changes—including increased dopamine release, dendritic branching in accumbens neurons, and altered gene expression—that are remarkably persistent, lasting months to years after cessation. This means that even after prolonged abstinence, encountering drug-associated cues can trigger intense 'wanting' surges. The sensitized system is like a loaded spring: the individual may not crave in the abstract, but exposure to the right cue at the right moment can unleash compulsive motivation.

Q3: If 'wanting' and 'liking' are dissociable, what does this imply for the design of recovery interventions?

It implies that interventions targeting conscious pleasure or rational evaluation of drug effects will be insufficient. The 'wanting' system operates largely below conscious awareness and is triggered by Pavlovian cue associations. Effective interventions must either reduce cue-triggered 'wanting' (e.g., through extinction, cue exposure therapy, or pharmacological dampening of dopamine reactivity), or

strengthen competing 'wanting' for non-drug rewards (which is essentially what contingency management does). Simply understanding that the drug is no longer pleasurable does not extinguish the motivational pull.

2. George F. Koob

National Institute on Alcohol Abuse and Alcoholism (NIAAA), NIH; formerly Scripps Research Institute | Neuropharmacology / Behavioral Neuroscience

Semantic Frame: Allostatic Model — Opponent Process & Anti-Reward Systems

Seminal Work: Koob, G.F. & Le Moal, M. (2001). 'Drug addiction, dysregulation of reward, and allostasis.' Neuropsychopharmacology, 24(2), 97–129.

Synthesis: Koob's allostatic model reframes addiction as a spiraling dysregulation of brain reward and stress systems. Initial drug use activates reward circuits (the a-process), but counteradaptive opponent processes (the b-process) engage to restore homeostasis. With chronic stimulant use, these opponent processes—mediated by corticotropin-releasing factor (CRF), norepinephrine, and dynorphin in the extended amygdala—fail to return to baseline, establishing a new, dysphoric 'allostatic state.' The individual now uses drugs not for euphoria but to alleviate the persistent negative emotional state of withdrawal. This model explains the three-stage addiction cycle: binge/intoxication, withdrawal/negative affect, and preoccupation/anticipation.

Q1: What distinguishes the allostatic model from simple tolerance, and why does this distinction matter for amphetamine recovery?

Tolerance implies a simple reduction in drug effect that reverses with abstinence. Allostasis describes a permanent shift in the hedonic set point: the brain's reward thermostat has been recalibrated. For amphetamine users, this means that even after the acute withdrawal syndrome resolves, a protracted state of anhedonia, dysphoria, and stress hyperreactivity persists—sometimes for months. Recovery must address this 'reward deficit' state, not just the absence of acute withdrawal.

Q2: How does the recruitment of anti-reward stress systems specifically explain the relapse vulnerability of stimulant users?

Chronic amphetamine use sensitizes the brain's stress systems—particularly CRF circuits in the central nucleus of the amygdala—such that they remain hyperactive during abstinence. Any stressor can now trigger disproportionate negative affect, which the individual has learned can be temporarily relieved by drug use. This is negative reinforcement: using to escape misery, not to seek pleasure. The stress-sensitization persists well beyond acute withdrawal, creating a window of vulnerability that standard time-limited treatment programs often fail to cover.

Q3: If addiction represents an allostatic state rather than a homeostatic perturbation, can the reward system ever fully 'reset,' or is recovery inherently a managed condition?

The evidence is nuanced. Volkow's neuroimaging work shows partial recovery of dopamine transporter density after 12–17 months of methamphetamine abstinence, suggesting some neural recovery is possible. However, the allostatic framework predicts that the anti-reward systems may never fully normalize—the 'zero prime' set point may be permanently shifted. This supports conceptualizing recovery as an ongoing active process rather than a cure, much like managing hypertension. The individual can achieve stability, but the underlying vulnerability persists, requiring sustained environmental support and

behavioral strategies.

3. Nora D. Volkow

National Institute on Drug Abuse (NIDA), NIH | Psychiatry / Neuroimaging / Neuropharmacology

Semantic Frame: Dopaminergic Neuroimaging — Addiction as Impaired Self-Regulation

Seminal Work: Volkow, N.D. et al. (2001). 'Loss of dopamine transporters in methamphetamine abusers recovers with protracted abstinence.' Journal of Neuroscience, 21(23), 9414–9418. Also: Volkow, N.D. & Morales, M. (2015). 'The brain on drugs: From reward to addiction.' Cell, 162(2).

Synthesis: Volkow pioneered the use of PET imaging to demonstrate that stimulant addiction produces measurable, specific changes in the human dopamine system. Her work showed that methamphetamine abusers have significantly reduced striatal dopamine transporter (DAT) density, that this reduction partially recovers with protracted abstinence (12–17 months), and that dopamine changes in the prefrontal cortex impair the circuits responsible for self-control, salience attribution, and decision-making. Her overarching contribution has been to establish addiction as a brain disorder characterized by the interaction between a hypersensitized reward/motivation system and a compromised prefrontal control system.

Q1: What is the empirical evidence that the methamphetamine-damaged dopamine system can recover, and what are the limits of that recovery?

PET studies show that dopamine transporter levels in the striatum, which are significantly reduced in active methamphetamine users, show substantial recovery after 12–17 months of sustained abstinence—approaching but not fully reaching healthy control levels. However, brain metabolic activity, particularly in the thalamus, does not recover to the same extent. And neuropsychological function improves only partially, suggesting that DAT recovery is necessary but not sufficient for full cognitive restoration. The implication is hopeful but sobering: the brain can heal, but the healing is incomplete and slow.

Q2: How does the impairment of prefrontal cortical function create the phenomenology of 'loss of control' in stimulant addiction?

Stimulant-induced dopamine dysregulation specifically degrades the orbitofrontal cortex (which maintains attention to goals and monitors their value) and the anterior cingulate cortex (which mediates conflict monitoring and action selection). When these circuits are compromised, the individual cannot effectively override the motivational pull of drug cues, cannot adequately weigh long-term consequences against immediate gratification, and cannot flexibly shift attention away from drug-related stimuli. It is not a failure of willpower but a measurable deficit in the neural hardware of executive control.

Q3: How should this neuroimaging evidence reshape public policy and clinical practice around stimulant addiction?

It demolishes the moral model of addiction. When you can show a person their brain scan and demonstrate that the circuits for self-control are physically impaired, it reframes addiction from a character defect to a medical condition. Clinically, it argues for extended treatment durations—the brain needs 12+ months of abstinence for significant recovery—and for interventions that specifically support prefrontal function (cognitive remediation, exercise, structured decision-making environments). It also argues strongly against purely punitive approaches, which stress a system that is already in stress-mediated dysregulation.

4. Rita Z. Goldstein

Icahn School of Medicine at Mount Sinai, Department of Psychiatry | Neuropsychology / Cognitive Neuroscience

Semantic Frame: Impaired Response Inhibition and Salience Attribution (iRISA)

Seminal Work: Goldstein, R.Z. & Volkow, N.D. (2002). 'Drug addiction and its underlying neurobiological basis: Neuroimaging evidence for the involvement of the frontal cortex.' American Journal of Psychiatry, 159(10), 1642–1652. Updated (2011).

Synthesis: The iRISA model integrates two complementary deficits: hyperactive salience attribution to drug-related stimuli (driven by sensitized striatal-limbic circuits) and impaired inhibitory control over behavior (driven by hypoactive prefrontal cortex). Goldstein's contribution was to formalize this as a single neurocognitive syndrome with specific, testable neuroimaging signatures. In stimulant users, drug cues activate the ventral striatum and amygdala more strongly than in controls, while stop-signal and go/no-go tasks reveal diminished prefrontal activation. The model predicts that effective treatment must address both sides: dampen drug-cue salience and restore executive inhibition.

Q1: What makes the iRISA model a more complete account of stimulant addiction than either the incentive sensitization or habit models alone?

Incentive sensitization explains the motivational pull but not the failure to resist it. Habit models explain automaticity but not the intense subjective craving. iRISA integrates both: it posits that addiction is the conjunction of excessive salience (the push toward drug use) and deficient inhibition (the inability to pull back). This dual-deficit model better explains the clinical heterogeneity of stimulant addiction—some individuals are primarily 'salience-driven' while others are primarily 'control-impaired'—and suggests that treatment should be matched to the individual's neurocognitive profile.

Q2: How does the salience attribution component manifest specifically in amphetamine vs. other substance use disorders?

Amphetamines, more than most other substances, produce profound and lasting changes in dopamine-mediated salience processing. The sensitization of D1 receptor signaling in the nucleus accumbens makes amphetamine-related cues disproportionately salient—they literally capture attention more strongly than competing stimuli. Neuroimaging shows that this hyper-salience persists well into abstinence and correlates with relapse vulnerability. This is why environmental cue management is particularly critical for stimulant recovery.

Q3: What are the clinical implications of the inhibitory control deficit for treatment timing and modality selection?

The inhibitory control deficit suggests that purely insight-based therapies (psychodynamic, motivational) may be insufficient during early recovery, when prefrontal function is most impaired. Early treatment should emphasize structured environmental support, contingency management (which bypasses the need for self-regulation by providing external reinforcement), and potentially cognitive remediation or neuromodulation to rebuild executive function. As prefrontal recovery progresses—typically over months of abstinence—the individual becomes increasingly capable of benefiting from cognitive-behavioral and insight-oriented approaches.

5. Eric J. Nestler

Icahn School of Medicine at Mount Sinai, Friedman Brain Institute | Molecular Psychiatry / Epigenetics

Semantic Frame: Molecular & Epigenetic Neuroplasticity of Addiction

Seminal Work: Nestler, E.J. (2001). 'Molecular basis of long-term plasticity underlying addiction.' Nature Reviews Neuroscience, 2, 119–128. Also: 'Epigenetic mechanisms of drug addiction' (2014).

Synthesis: Nestler's work elucidated the molecular cascades through which repeated stimulant exposure produces lasting changes in gene expression, chromatin structure, and synaptic morphology in the mesolimbic reward circuit. His research on the transcription factor DeltaFosB demonstrated that this protein accumulates in the nucleus accumbens with chronic drug use, persists for weeks after cessation, and directly modifies the expression of genes involved in synaptic plasticity and reward sensitivity. This provided the first clear molecular mechanism for the long-lasting nature of addiction vulnerability.

Q1: What role does DeltaFosB play as a molecular switch in the transition from recreational stimulant use to addiction?

DeltaFosB is an unusually stable transcription factor that accumulates gradually with repeated drug exposure and, once present, alters the expression of downstream genes that increase sensitivity to drug rewards and enhance the motivation to seek them. It is essentially a molecular memory of chronic drug exposure—a switch that, once thrown, is slow to reset. In the context of amphetamines, the dopamine surge produced by each use drives DeltaFosB accumulation, progressively rewriting the transcriptional landscape of accumbens neurons to favor drug-seeking behavior.

Q2: How do epigenetic modifications extend the timeline of addiction vulnerability beyond what pharmacological models alone predict?

Epigenetic changes—histone acetylation, DNA methylation, chromatin remodeling—can persist for months or years after the last drug exposure, long after the drug itself has cleared the system. These modifications do not change the DNA sequence but alter which genes are accessible for transcription. This explains why addiction vulnerability persists well beyond the pharmacokinetic half-life of the drug: the chromatin landscape of reward neurons has been physically restructured. It also opens new therapeutic avenues through histone deacetylase inhibitors and other epigenetic modulators.

Q3: What does molecular-level evidence suggest about the theoretical possibility of 'curing' versus 'managing' stimulant addiction?

The molecular evidence paints a picture of deep, durable neural change. While some modifications reverse with abstinence, others—particularly epigenetic changes and structural remodeling of dendritic spines—persist indefinitely. This does not mean recovery is impossible, but it does mean that the recovered brain is not identical to the pre-addiction brain. Recovery involves building new circuits and behaviors on top of altered architecture, rather than restoring a pristine original state. This is why environmental restructuring and ongoing behavioral support matter so much—they provide the scaffolding that compensates for permanently altered substrates.

#### Cluster B: LEARNING, HABIT & COMPUTATIONAL FRAMES

6. Barry J. Everitt

University of Cambridge, Department of Psychology (Emeritus) | Behavioral Neuroscience / Learning Theory

Semantic Frame: Habit-Compulsion Transition

Seminal Work: Everitt, B.J. & Robbins, T.W. (2005). 'Neural systems of reinforcement for drug addiction: From actions to habits to compulsion.' Nature Neuroscience, 8(11), 1481–1489.

Synthesis: Everitt and Robbins proposed that drug addiction represents a transition from voluntary, goal-directed drug use to habitual and ultimately compulsive drug seeking. This transition is neurally underpinned by a shift from prefrontal cortical to striatal control over behavior, and from ventral to dorsal striatal dominance—mediated by the serial connectivity of these regions through midbrain dopamine projections. Using sophisticated second-order reinforcement schedules in rats, they demonstrated that well-established cocaine-seeking behavior becomes resistant to outcome devaluation (a hallmark of habit) and depends on dorsolateral striatal dopamine rather than ventral striatal circuitry.

Q1: How does the actions-to-habits-to-compulsions framework explain why stimulant users persist in drug seeking even when they report no longer enjoying the drug?

Once drug seeking has become habitual—stamped in as a stimulus-response association in the dorsal striatum—it no longer requires the drug's hedonic value to sustain it. The behavior is triggered automatically by environmental cues, just as a person might find themselves walking to their office on a weekend morning out of habit. But habit alone is not compulsion. Compulsion emerges when habitual drug seeking is combined with impaired prefrontal 'top-down' control—the individual cannot override the automatic behavior even when they recognize its harmful consequences.

Q2: What distinguishes the habit model from the incentive sensitization model, and are they truly competing accounts?

They are complementary rather than competing. Incentive sensitization explains the motivational 'push'—the cue-triggered wanting that initiates drug seeking. The habit model explains the automaticity of the behavioral response once initiated. In practice, both processes operate in the same individual: sensitized wanting triggers the urge, and habitual motor programs execute the drug-seeking sequence without deliberate intention. The clinical implication is that treatment must address both the motivational and the habitual components.

Q3: What does this model imply for the optimal timing and sequencing of recovery interventions?

The model suggests that early recovery, when drug-seeking habits are strongest and prefrontal control is most impaired, should prioritize environmental restructuring (removing cues that trigger habitual sequences) and contingency management (providing alternative reinforcement for non-drug behavior). As prefrontal function recovers with sustained abstinence, the individual becomes increasingly capable of deploying goal-directed strategies—cognitive behavioral techniques, values-based decision making—that can compete with and eventually override habitual drug-seeking patterns.

7. Trevor W. Robbins

University of Cambridge, Department of Psychology | Cognitive Neuroscience / Neuropsychopharmacology

Semantic Frame: Impulsivity-Compulsivity Endophenotype Spectrum

Seminal Work: Robbins, T.W., Gillan, C.M., Smith, D.G., de Wit, S. & Ersche, K.D. (2012). 'Neurocognitive endophenotypes of impulsivity and compulsivity.' Current Opinion in Neurobiology, 22(6).

Synthesis: Robbins extended the habit-compulsion framework to incorporate individual vulnerability factors, demonstrating that pre-existing traits of impulsivity and compulsivity—measurable through neurocognitive tasks and associated with distinct neural signatures—serve as endophenotypes for stimulant addiction. Working with Karen Ersche and others, his group showed that unaffected siblings of stimulant-dependent individuals share neurocognitive impairments in response inhibition and increased gray matter in the putamen, suggesting that these traits precede and predispose to addiction rather than merely resulting from drug exposure.

Q1: What evidence distinguishes pre-existing neurocognitive vulnerability from drug-induced impairment in stimulant addiction?

The sibling studies are decisive. Unaffected biological siblings of cocaine/amphetamine-dependent individuals show the same pattern of impaired stop-signal inhibition and abnormal fronto-striatal structure as their addicted siblings—despite never having developed addiction themselves. This demonstrates that the neurocognitive profile is not merely a consequence of drug exposure but a pre-existing vulnerability trait that is shared within families, presumably through genetic and/or early environmental pathways.

Q2: How does the impulsivity-compulsivity dimension map onto the stages of stimulant addiction?

High trait impulsivity facilitates initial experimentation and early escalation—impulsive individuals are more likely to try drugs, more likely to take higher doses, and less able to regulate intake. As addiction progresses, compulsivity traits become more relevant: the individual develops rigid, stereotyped drug-seeking patterns that persist despite punishment and resist flexible updating. This dimensional mapping suggests that impulsivity-focused interventions (delay of gratification training, working memory enhancement) may be most relevant in early stages, while compulsivity-focused interventions (habit-breaking, exposure-response prevention) become more critical later.

Q3: What are the implications of endophenotype research for prevention and early identification of stimulant addiction risk?

If we can identify neurocognitive profiles that predict addiction vulnerability before drug exposure occurs, we can target prevention efforts more precisely. A young person showing high trait impulsivity, poor response inhibition, and a family history of stimulant use disorder could be offered targeted interventions—executive function training, environmental enrichment, mentoring—before addiction develops. This shifts the paradigm from reactive treatment to proactive prevention, grounded in measurable neurobehavioral risk markers.

8. A. David Redish

University of Minnesota, Department of Neuroscience | Computational Neuroscience

Semantic Frame: Computational Reinforcement Learning Model of Addiction

Seminal Work: Redish, A.D. (2004). 'Addiction as a computational process gone awry.' Science, 306(5703), 1944–1947.

Synthesis: Redish formalized the computational account of addiction as a disorder of the reinforcement learning system. Drawing on temporal difference (TD) learning models, he demonstrated how addictive drugs produce aberrant prediction error signals—pharmacologically amplified dopamine surges that overwrite the normal updating of reward values. Drugs effectively 'hack' the reward prediction error signal to teach the brain that drugs are far more valuable than they actually are, and this inflated value persists because the pharmacological override bypasses the normal correction mechanisms that would eventually diminish value through satiation or negative consequences.

Q1: How does the pharmacological 'hijacking' of prediction error signals differ qualitatively from the normal process of learning to like a natural reward?

Natural rewards produce a prediction error that diminishes as the reward becomes expected—this is how the system learns accurate values and prevents over-pursuit of any single reward. Addictive drugs bypass this self-correcting mechanism by directly activating dopamine release independent of actual outcome evaluation. Each dose produces a fresh, pharmacologically-driven prediction error signal regardless of how many times the drug has been taken. The system never 'learns' the drug's true value because the teaching signal is being artificially generated rather than computed from experience.

Q2: What does this computational account predict about the specific failure modes of decision-making in stimulant addiction?

It predicts several specific deficits: over-valuation of drug rewards (inflated learned values), under-valuation of non-drug alternatives (because normal prediction errors are comparatively weak), failure to update drug value downward in response to negative consequences (because the pharmacological override overwhelms negative prediction errors), and temporal myopia (because the immediate pharmacological signal dominates delayed consequence signals). Each of these predictions has been confirmed in behavioral studies with stimulant-dependent populations.

Q3: How might computational models inform the design of 'precision' recovery interventions for stimulant addiction?

If we can computationally fingerprint an individual's specific pattern of value distortion—whether they primarily over-value drugs, under-value alternatives, fail to learn from punishment, or discount the future excessively—we can match interventions to their specific computational deficit. Someone with primarily inflated drug valuation might benefit most from extinction-based approaches; someone with suppressed alternative valuation might benefit most from behavioral activation and contingency management; someone with temporal discounting might benefit from episodic future thinking interventions. The computational framework provides the diagnostic taxonomy for personalized treatment.

9. Warren K. Bickel

Virginia Tech Carilion Research Institute, Fralin Biomedical Research Institute | Behavioral Economics / Neuroeconomics

Semantic Frame: Temporal Discounting & Competing Neurobehavioral Decision Systems

Seminal Work: Bickel, W.K. et al. (2007). 'Behavioral and neuroeconomics of drug addiction.' Current Drug Abuse Reviews, 1(1). Also: Bickel, W.K. et al. (2014). 'The behavioral economics and neuroeconomics of

reinforcer pathologies.' Annual Review of Clinical Psychology, 10.

Synthesis: Bickel established temporal discounting—the tendency to prefer smaller immediate rewards over larger delayed ones—as a trans-diagnostic marker of addiction. His Competing Neurobehavioral Decision Systems (CNDS) framework proposes that addiction reflects a dominance of the impulsive (limbic) system over the executive (prefrontal) system in intertemporal choice. For stimulant users, this manifests as extraordinarily steep discounting of future rewards, meaning the immediate dopamine hit from amphetamine use massively outweighs the perceived value of any future benefit from abstinence.

Q1: How does temporal discounting function as a mechanism rather than merely a correlate of stimulant addiction?

Temporal discounting is both a vulnerability factor and a consequence of drug use, creating a vicious cycle. Pre-existing steep discounting predisposes individuals to choose immediate drug rewards over delayed sobriety benefits. But chronic stimulant use further impairs prefrontal executive function, steepening discounting further. This creates a trap: the very drug that exploits the discounting bias also worsens it, progressively narrowing the temporal window within which consequences can influence behavior. Interventions that flatten the discounting curve—such as episodic future thinking, where individuals vividly imagine future positive outcomes—can break this cycle.

Q2: What does the Competing Neurobehavioral Decision Systems framework predict about when and why stimulant users will relapse?

The CNDS framework predicts that relapse is most likely when the executive system is temporarily depleted or offline—during stress, cognitive fatigue, hunger, or sleep deprivation—and the impulsive system encounters drug cues in the absence of executive oversight. This explains the classic relapse scenarios: the person in early recovery who encounters a former using partner late at night when tired and stressed. The framework prescribes that recovery environments should minimize these vulnerability states and provide contingency management during the windows when executive function is lowest.

Q3: How does behavioral economics bridge the gap between neuroscience and policy in stimulant addiction treatment?

Behavioral economics provides the theoretical justification for contingency management in a language that policymakers understand: incentive design. If addiction is fundamentally a choice disorder driven by steep temporal discounting, then the remedy is to restructure the choice architecture—make the immediate rewards for abstinence tangible and competitive with the immediate rewards of drug use. This is precisely what contingency management does. The behavioral economic framework also explains why punishment-based approaches fail: they add delayed negative consequences to a population that, by definition, is insensitive to delayed consequences.

10. Karen D. Ersche

University of Cambridge, Department of Psychiatry | Neuropsychology / Addiction Neuroscience

Semantic Frame: Stimulant Addiction Endophenotype

Seminal Work: Ersche, K.D. et al. (2012). 'Abnormal brain structure implicated in stimulant drug addiction.' Science, 335(6068), 601–604.

Synthesis: Ersche demonstrated that both stimulant-dependent individuals and their unaffected biological siblings share structural brain abnormalities—enlarged putamen, reduced inferior frontal gyrus volume, and impaired white matter integrity—that predispose to addiction. This landmark study in Science established that key neural features of stimulant addiction are not simply consequences of drug exposure but pre-existing endophenotypes. Her computational modeling work has further shown that stimulant users exhibit reduced punishment learning rates and increased behavioral 'stickiness,' suggesting specific algorithmic deficits in flexible decision-making.

Q1: What does the finding of shared brain abnormalities in unaffected siblings fundamentally change about how we understand the etiology of stimulant addiction?

It dismantles the assumption that brain differences in addiction are purely drug-induced damage. The siblings study reveals that the brains of individuals who go on to develop stimulant addiction were already different before they ever used drugs. This shifts the etiological narrative from 'drugs cause brain disease' to 'pre-existing brain variation creates vulnerability that drugs exploit.' The practical implication is enormous: it means prevention can be targeted at individuals with identifiable risk profiles, and treatment can be designed to compensate for specific pre-existing deficits rather than solely repairing drug-induced damage.

Q2: How does the concept of reduced punishment sensitivity manifest in the real-world behavior of amphetamine users?

Reduced punishment sensitivity means that negative consequences—job loss, relationship breakdown, legal trouble—have less impact on behavioral updating than they would in non-vulnerable individuals. The stimulant user continues to make the same choices not because they are unaware of the consequences but because their learning system gives less weight to punishment signals. This explains the frustrating clinical observation that 'hitting rock bottom' is less effective as a catalyst for change than many believe: the neural system for learning from negative outcomes is itself impaired.

Q3: What are the implications of endophenotype research for the ADHD-stimulant addiction overlap?

This is a critical question given the amphetamine focus. ADHD and stimulant addiction share overlapping neurocognitive profiles—impulsivity, executive dysfunction, altered striatal structure—and both respond to the same pharmacological agent (amphetamine), albeit at different doses and in different contexts. The endophenotype framework suggests they may share common genetic and neural substrates, with ADHD representing a milder expression of the same vulnerability that, in the presence of additional risk factors, manifests as addiction. This has important implications for the prescribing of stimulant medications to ADHD populations.

#### Cluster C: CLINICAL INTERVENTION & RECOVERY FRAMES

11. Stephen T. Higgins

University of Vermont, Vermont Center on Behavior and Health | Behavioral Pharmacology / Clinical Psychology

Semantic Frame: Voucher-Based Contingency Management

Seminal Work: Higgins, S.T. et al. (1994). 'Incentives improve outcome in outpatient behavioral treatment of cocaine dependence.' Archives of General Psychiatry, 51(7), 568–576.

Synthesis: Higgins developed and validated the voucher-based reinforcement therapy (VBRT) approach, demonstrating that providing escalating monetary vouchers contingent on drug-negative urine samples dramatically improved cocaine/stimulant abstinence rates compared to standard treatment. His systematic research program established the core principles: immediacy of reinforcement, escalating value schedules, and reset contingencies. Multiple meta-analyses have since confirmed contingency management as the most effective behavioral treatment for stimulant use disorders, with effect sizes surpassing those of cognitive-behavioral therapy.

Q1: Why does contingency management work better for stimulant addiction than for most other substance use disorders, and better than any other behavioral therapy?

Stimulant addiction is characterized by a profound devaluation of non-drug reinforcers. The dopaminergic sensitization produced by amphetamines makes drug rewards disproportionately salient while natural rewards fade into motivational insignificance. Contingency management works by creating an artificial but tangible competing reinforcer—money, prizes—that can compete with drug rewards in the immediate temporal window that matters to the stimulant user's steeply discounting decision system. It does not require intact executive function, insight, or motivation to work—it operates at the level of operant reinforcement, which is precisely the level at which stimulant addiction is maintained.

Q2: What are the primary barriers to widespread implementation of contingency management, and how should the field address them?

Three barriers dominate. First, philosophical resistance: many clinicians and policymakers view 'paying people to stop using drugs' as morally objectionable, reflecting a persistent folk-psychological commitment to the idea that recovery should require suffering or willpower. Second, cost and reimbursement: the direct costs of incentives ($300–$1,200 per patient per treatment episode), combined with the absence of third-party reimbursement in most systems, make implementation financially challenging. Third, regulatory constraints: until recently, federal limits on incentive values ($75 per individual) severely constrained the therapeutic dose. Each barrier requires a different strategy: education for the first, health-economic modeling for the second, and policy advocacy for the third.

Q3: Does the effectiveness of contingency management persist after the incentives are removed, and what does this tell us about the nature of recovery?

This is the central critique, and the evidence is honestly mixed. Most studies show that the abstinence gains during active contingency management partially erode after incentives cease. However, several important caveats apply: longer treatment durations produce more durable effects; individuals who achieve extended abstinence during CM show better long-term outcomes regardless of CM cessation; and the period of enforced abstinence allows neural recovery that may support subsequent self-regulation. The deeper lesson is that contingency management should not be conceptualized as a

stand-alone cure but as a scaffold that maintains abstinence while the brain heals and while other recovery capital accumulates.

12. Nancy M. Petry (1964–2018)

University of Connecticut Health Center (posthumous) | Clinical Psychology / Behavioral Economics

Semantic Frame: Prize-Based Contingency Management

Seminal Work: Petry, N.M. et al. (2005). 'Effect of prize-based incentives on outcomes in stimulant abusers in outpatient psychosocial treatment programs.' Archives of General Psychiatry, 62(10), 1148–1156.

Synthesis: Petry developed the prize-based or 'fishbowl' contingency management method, which uses variable-ratio reinforcement schedules to deliver prizes of varying magnitude—from verbal praise to high-value items—contingent on drug-negative urine samples. This innovation dramatically reduced the per-patient cost of contingency management while maintaining its efficacy, making large-scale implementation more feasible. The NIDA Clinical Trials Network multi-site MIEDAR study validated this approach across diverse community treatment settings, establishing the prize-based method as the most implementation-ready CM model.

Q1: How does the variable-ratio reinforcement schedule in prize-based CM exploit principles from behavioral economics to maximize engagement?

Variable-ratio schedules produce the highest and most persistent response rates of any reinforcement schedule—this is the same principle that makes slot machines so compelling. By making each drug-negative urine sample an opportunity to 'win,' prize-based CM harnesses the same probabilistic reward mechanisms that contribute to addictive behavior in gambling and redirects them toward pro-recovery behavior. The uncertainty of the prize value creates anticipatory dopamine release—essentially using the brain's own reward system to reinforce abstinence rather than drug use.

Q2: What was Petry's contribution to resolving the ethical and practical objections to contingency management?

Petry addressed the cost objection directly by demonstrating that a variable-ratio prize system could produce equivalent abstinence outcomes at a fraction of the cost of voucher-based approaches. She also generated the implementation science evidence—showing that prize CM could be delivered by front-line clinical staff in community settings without specialized training. Perhaps most importantly, she reframed the ethical objection: she argued that it is more ethically problematic to withhold the most effective treatment for stimulant addiction than to provide financial incentives for health behavior change, a practice already accepted in wellness programs, medication adherence interventions, and public health campaigns.

Q3: What lessons does the contingency management literature offer for designing broader 'personal operating systems' for addiction recovery?

The core lesson is that the immediate reinforcement landscape matters more than insight, motivation, or knowledge. A well-designed recovery operating system must engineer the individual's immediate environment to provide tangible, timely reinforcement for each pro-recovery micro-behavior—attending an appointment, completing an exercise session, maintaining a sleep schedule—not just for the macro-behavior of abstinence. The CM literature also teaches that escalating reinforcement contingencies are more effective than fixed ones, that reset contingencies (rather than total loss) maintain

engagement after lapses, and that pairing abstinence incentives with goal-directed activities produces more durable outcomes than incentivizing abstinence alone.

13. Judson A. Brewer

Brown University, Mindfulness Center (formerly Yale, MIT) | Psychiatry / Contemplative Neuroscience

Semantic Frame: Mindfulness-Based Operant Deconditioning of Craving

Seminal Work: Brewer, J.A., Elwafi, H.M. & Davis, J.H. (2013). 'Craving to quit: Psychological models and neurobiological mechanisms of mindfulness training as treatment for addictions.' Psychology of Addictive Behaviors, 27(2), 366–379.

Synthesis: Brewer integrated Buddhist models of craving (tanha) with operant conditioning theory and neuroimaging to explain why mindfulness training can interrupt the addictive loop. His central insight is that standard cognitive-behavioral approaches (avoidance of cues, substitution of behaviors) leave the core operant loop intact—the association between affective trigger, craving, and use behavior is merely circumvented, not dismantled. Mindfulness training targets the craving-behavior link directly by teaching individuals to observe craving as a transient sensory-affective phenomenon rather than an imperative to act, effectively decoupling the urge from the habitual response.

Q1: How does the mindfulness model of addiction differ from the cognitive-behavioral model at the level of the operant loop?

CBT strategies like cue avoidance and cognitive restructuring work 'outside' the loop—they try to prevent the individual from entering the loop (by avoiding cues) or to add cognitive counter-arguments once inside it (by challenging drug-positive thoughts). But the loop itself—the learned association between affective state, craving, and relief through use—remains intact, leaving the individual perpetually vulnerable to relapse when avoidance fails. Mindfulness operates 'inside' the loop: it changes the individual's relationship to the craving itself, so that the craving arises but does not automatically trigger the behavioral response. This is disenchantment through direct observation rather than suppression through cognitive effort.

Q2: What neurobiological evidence supports the claim that mindfulness training modifies the craving-behavior link in the brain?

Neuroimaging studies from our group and others show that mindfulness training reduces activity in the posterior cingulate cortex (a hub of the default mode network associated with self-referential processing and craving) and decouples activity between the subgenual cingulate and striatum during craving states. In smokers, mindfulness training produced a dissociation between craving and smoking behavior—participants continued to experience craving but smoked less, suggesting that the link between urge and action was weakened. Real-time fMRI neurofeedback studies further show that individuals can learn to regulate PCC activity volitionally, and that this regulation correlates with reduced craving intensity.

Q3: How applicable is the mindfulness approach to methamphetamine addiction specifically, given the severity of neurocognitive impairment in this population?

This is an important and honest concern. Methamphetamine users present with more severe attention deficits, emotion dysregulation, and prefrontal impairment than many other substance-using populations. Standard 8-week mindfulness programs may be too demanding for individuals in early methamphetamine

recovery. However, adapted protocols—shorter sessions, more concrete and somatic (rather than cognitive) mindfulness exercises, integration with contingency management to maintain engagement—show promise. The key insight is that mindfulness for stimulant addiction should target interoceptive awareness of craving states, which is a more basic and accessible skill than the sustained attention required for breath-focused meditation.

14. Sarah Bowen

Pacific University, formerly University of Washington | Clinical Psychology / Addiction Treatment

Semantic Frame: Mindfulness-Based Relapse Prevention (MBRP)

Seminal Work: Bowen, S. et al. (2014). 'Relative efficacy of mindfulness-based relapse prevention, standard relapse prevention, and treatment as usual for substance use disorders.' JAMA Psychiatry, 71(5), 547–556.

Synthesis: Bowen formalized and manualized Mindfulness-Based Relapse Prevention (MBRP), combining traditional relapse prevention strategies with mindfulness meditation practices. Her landmark JAMA Psychiatry trial demonstrated that MBRP outperformed both standard relapse prevention and treatment-as-usual on drug use outcomes at 12-month follow-up, with the advantage emerging specifically at the 6–12 month time point. This delayed-emergence effect suggests that mindfulness-based approaches build cumulative self-regulatory capacity that becomes increasingly protective over time, in contrast to standard RP skills that may decay without practice.

Q1: What is the theoretical mechanism that explains MBRP's delayed but superior efficacy compared to standard relapse prevention?

Standard relapse prevention teaches specific coping strategies for identified high-risk situations. These strategies are effective in the short term but require the individual to correctly identify the risk situation and retrieve the appropriate strategy—a process vulnerable to cognitive load, stress, and the very executive dysfunction that characterizes addiction. MBRP teaches a meta-skill: the capacity to observe one's own internal states without automatic reactivity. This meta-skill generalizes across situations, does not depend on specific cue identification, and actually strengthens with practice over time. The delayed effect reflects the gradual internalization of this meta-cognitive capacity.

Q2: How does MBRP address the relationship between negative affect and craving, which is particularly intense in stimulant withdrawal?

MBRP specifically trains individuals to 'sit with' negative affective states—the dysphoria, anxiety, and irritability that characterize protracted stimulant withdrawal—without engaging in the habitual response of seeking relief through drug use. The program teaches 'urge surfing': the practice of observing craving as a wave that rises, peaks, and subsides on its own without the individual needing to act on it. For stimulant recovery, where the protracted withdrawal phase can last months, this capacity to tolerate negative affect without reactivity is arguably the single most important self-regulatory skill.

Q3: What adaptations of MBRP would be most important for a population specifically recovering from amphetamine use disorder?

Three key adaptations: First, extend the program beyond 8 weeks to match the protracted timeline of stimulant recovery. Second, incorporate body-based practices (body scan, walking meditation) earlier and more prominently, as these are more accessible than breath-focused meditation for individuals with

stimulant-induced attention deficits. Third, integrate contingency management reinforcement for meditation practice—not just for abstinence—to support habit formation around the mindfulness practice itself during the period when intrinsic motivation may be insufficient.

15. Kathleen T. Carroll

Yale University School of Medicine, Department of Psychiatry | Clinical Psychology / Treatment Development

Semantic Frame: Computer-Based CBT & Combined Treatment Platforms

Seminal Work: Carroll, K.M. et al. (2008). 'CBT4CBT: Computer-based training in cognitive-behavioral therapy as a treatment for substance use disorders.' American Journal of Psychiatry, 165(7), 881–888.

Synthesis: Carroll has been a leader in developing and testing cognitive-behavioral approaches for stimulant addiction, with particular focus on making evidence-based treatments scalable and accessible. Her CBT4CBT platform (computer-based training in CBT) demonstrated that machine-delivered CBT could produce durable effects on stimulant use outcomes, particularly when combined with contingency management. Her work has been instrumental in demonstrating that combination treatments—pairing behavioral therapies with one another and with pharmacotherapy—produce better outcomes than monotherapy for stimulant use disorders.

Q1: Why has the combination of CBT and contingency management proven more effective than either alone for stimulant addiction?

They target complementary mechanisms through different temporal windows. Contingency management provides immediate reinforcement for abstinence during the acute phase when executive function is impaired and long-term thinking is compromised. CBT builds the cognitive skill set—functional analysis, coping strategy repertoire, relapse prevention planning—that sustains recovery after CM incentives end. CM 'holds' the patient in abstinence while CBT 'teaches' them how to maintain it. Without CM, many patients leave treatment before CBT skills can be acquired; without CBT, the skills needed for long-term maintenance are never learned.

Q2: What does the success of computerized CBT delivery suggest about the future of scalable recovery interventions?

It demonstrates that the active ingredients of CBT can be extracted from the therapist-patient relationship and delivered through technology without losing efficacy—and in some studies, with enhanced durability compared to clinician-delivered CBT. This has enormous implications for the treatment gap in stimulant addiction, where there are far more individuals who need treatment than trained clinicians available to provide it. The logical extension is AI-assisted, adaptive treatment platforms that can deliver personalized CBT content in real time, adjusting to the individual's progress, risk level, and learning style.

Q3: How should treatment sequencing be optimized for amphetamine use disorder given the current evidence base?

The evidence suggests a phased approach: initiate with contingency management to establish abstinence and engage the patient; layer in CBT (potentially computer-delivered) as executive function recovers sufficiently to benefit from cognitive skill training; add pharmacotherapy (naltrexone/bupropion combination or emerging agents) where available; and transition to maintenance-phase interventions (MBRP, mutual aid, recovery coaching) for long-term relapse prevention. The key insight is that no single modality is sufficient across the entire recovery trajectory—the intervention must evolve as the brain and

the person recover.

16. William R. Miller

University of New Mexico (Emeritus) | Clinical Psychology

Semantic Frame: Motivational Interviewing

Seminal Work: Miller, W.R. & Rollnick, S. (1991/2013). 'Motivational Interviewing: Helping People Change.' (3rd ed.). Guilford Press.

Synthesis: Miller developed Motivational Interviewing (MI) as a client-centered, directive counseling approach that works with ambivalence about change rather than against it. Grounded in the observation that confrontational approaches increase resistance while empathic, autonomy-supportive approaches facilitate change talk, MI has become one of the most widely practiced therapeutic approaches in addiction treatment worldwide. Its application to stimulant use disorders has shown consistent moderate effects on treatment engagement and retention, and it serves as a foundation upon which more specific interventions are built.

Q1: How does motivational interviewing's mechanism of action relate to self-determination theory's account of autonomous motivation?

MI works by enhancing the individual's own autonomous motivation for change rather than imposing external motivational pressure. In self-determination theory terms, MI supports all three basic psychological needs: autonomy (by respecting the individual's right to choose), competence (by highlighting their existing strengths and past successes), and relatedness (through empathic, non-judgmental clinical relationship). The resulting 'change talk'—the individual's own voiced arguments for change—is a marker of autonomous motivation that predicts actual behavior change. This is precisely why MI works better than confrontation: externally imposed pressure produces identified or introjected motivation, which is less durable than autonomous motivation.

Q2: What role should MI play in the treatment of stimulant addiction, given that it shows moderate effects compared to contingency management?

MI is best conceptualized not as a primary treatment for stimulant addiction but as an engagement and preparation platform. Its greatest value is in bridging the gap between the moment someone presents for treatment and the point at which more intensive interventions can take hold. For stimulant users, who often present with high ambivalence and low treatment motivation, MI increases the probability that they will initiate and remain in treatment long enough to benefit from CM, CBT, or pharmacotherapy. It is the relational substrate upon which more specific interventions are constructed.

Q3: How does MI address the stigma and shame that are particularly pronounced in stimulant-using populations?

Stimulant users face intense stigma—societal, clinical, and self-directed. MI's non-judgmental, empathic stance directly counteracts the internalized shame that keeps many stimulant users from seeking help. By reflecting the individual's own values and aspirations back to them without imposing moral judgment, MI creates a therapeutic space where change becomes possible without first requiring self-condemnation. This is particularly important for methamphetamine users, who face compounded stigma related to sexuality, poverty, and mental health comorbidity.

17. Madhukar H. Trivedi

UT Southwestern Medical Center, Department of Psychiatry | Psychiatry / Clinical Trials

Semantic Frame: Pharmacological Combination Therapy for Stimulant Use Disorder

Seminal Work: Trivedi, M.H. et al. (2021). 'Trial of naltrexone-bupropion combination for methamphetamine use disorder.' New England Journal of Medicine, 384(2), 140–153.

Synthesis: Trivedi led the landmark NEJM trial demonstrating that injectable naltrexone combined with oral bupropion achieved significantly higher response rates than placebo in adults with moderate-to-severe methamphetamine use disorder. While the absolute response rate was modest (16.5% vs. 3.4%), this represented the first Phase III evidence of any pharmacological efficacy for stimulant addiction. The combination targets two complementary mechanisms: bupropion's dopamine/norepinephrine reuptake inhibition addresses the dysphoric withdrawal state, while naltrexone's opioid antagonism may modulate dopamine release and reduce craving.

Q1: What does a response rate of 16.5% tell us about the pharmacological tractability of stimulant addiction compared to other substance use disorders?

It tells us that stimulant addiction is pharmacologically harder to treat than opioid or alcohol use disorders, where approved medications achieve higher response rates. But the number needed to treat (NNT) of 9 is actually comparable to many accepted psychiatric treatments—including naltrexone for alcohol use disorder and antidepressants for depression. The modesty of the effect reflects not the failure of the specific medication but the complexity of the underlying neurobiology: stimulant addiction involves distributed changes across multiple neurotransmitter systems and brain circuits, making single-target pharmacology insufficient.

Q2: How does the combination of naltrexone and bupropion address the allostatic state described by Koob's model?

Bupropion partially restores dopaminergic and noradrenergic tone, which are depleted in the allostatic state, ameliorating the anhedonia and amotivation that drive negative reinforcement. Naltrexone blocks opioid receptors that contribute to the hedonic response to drug cues, potentially dampening cue-triggered craving. Together, they address both sides of Koob's equation: the reward deficit (bupropion raises the depressed baseline) and the anti-reward excess (naltrexone may attenuate stress-driven opioidergic dysregulation). It is a rationally designed combination, even if the effect is modest.

Q3: What does this trial suggest about the future direction of pharmacotherapy development for stimulant addiction?

It suggests that combination approaches targeting multiple mechanisms will be more productive than searching for a single 'magic bullet' medication. Future directions include immunotherapies (anti-methamphetamine antibodies), GLP-1 receptor agonists (which show preliminary promise for reducing stimulant use), and neuromodulation-pharmacotherapy combinations. The Trivedi trial also underscores the importance of pairing pharmacotherapy with behavioral interventions—the recommendation for future research is to test whether adding contingency management to naltrexone/bupropion produces additive or synergistic effects.

18. Colleen A. Hanlon

Wake Forest University School of Medicine | Neuroscience / Neuromodulation

Semantic Frame: Transcranial Magnetic Stimulation for Stimulant Addiction

Seminal Work: Hanlon, C.A. et al. (2015). 'Probing the frontostriatal loops involved in executive and limbic processing via interleaved TMS and functional MRI.' Journal of Neuroscience, 35(40).

Synthesis: Hanlon has been a pioneer in applying transcranial magnetic stimulation (TMS) to target the frontostriatal circuits impaired in stimulant addiction. Her research has used interleaved TMS-fMRI to map the causal connectivity between prefrontal cortex and striatum during craving and cognitive control tasks, and has developed protocols for therapeutic TMS applications. Her work represents the frontier of direct neural circuit intervention—the possibility of strengthening the very executive control circuits that stimulant use has weakened.

Q1: How does TMS differ from pharmacotherapy in its approach to restoring the impaired executive control circuits in stimulant addiction?

Pharmacotherapy modulates neurotransmitter levels broadly across the brain, producing both therapeutic and off-target effects. TMS can directly stimulate specific cortical targets—particularly the dorsolateral prefrontal cortex and the medial prefrontal cortex/anterior cingulate—with spatial and temporal precision. This allows for targeted strengthening of the exact circuits identified by neuroimaging as impaired in stimulant addiction. In principle, repeated TMS sessions can induce long-term potentiation-like synaptic changes in frontostriatal pathways, gradually rebuilding the executive control infrastructure that chronic stimulant use has degraded.

Q2: What are the current limitations and realistic timeline for TMS as a clinical tool for stimulant addiction treatment?

The primary limitations are: most studies to date are small, uncontrolled, or focused on cocaine rather than amphetamines specifically; optimal stimulation parameters (frequency, duration, target, number of sessions) have not been established through large comparative trials; and access is limited to specialized research centers. A realistic timeline is 5–10 years before TMS for stimulant addiction might achieve FDA clearance, assuming positive results from ongoing multi-site trials. In the meantime, it represents an important proof of concept that direct neural circuit modulation can complement behavioral and pharmacological approaches.

Q3: How might neuromodulation be integrated into a comprehensive recovery framework rather than deployed as a standalone treatment?

The most promising integration would be using TMS to 'prime' the prefrontal cortex before CBT or CM sessions—enhancing the executive resources available for skill learning and behavioral control. Another approach would be to use TMS during the critical early abstinence window, when prefrontal function is most impaired and relapse risk is highest, effectively serving as a 'neural bridge' that supports executive function until natural recovery processes take hold. The future likely involves personalized neuromodulation protocols guided by individual fMRI mapping of circuit dysfunction.

#### Cluster D: PSYCHOSOCIAL, ECOLOGICAL & IDENTITY FRAMES

19. William L. White

Chestnut Health Systems (Senior Research Consultant, Emeritus) | Addiction History / Recovery Science / Public Health

Semantic Frame: Recovery Capital & Recovery Management

Seminal Work: White, W.L. & Cloud, W. (2008). 'Recovery capital: A primer for addictions professionals.' Counselor, 9(5), 22–27. Also: White, W.L. (2007). 'Addiction recovery: Its definition and conceptual boundaries.' Journal of Substance Abuse Treatment.

Synthesis: White transformed the field's conception of recovery from a narrow focus on abstinence and acute treatment episodes to a comprehensive model of long-term recovery management grounded in the concept of 'recovery capital.' Drawing on Bourdieu's sociology, he defined recovery capital as the sum of internal and external resources that can be drawn upon to initiate and sustain recovery: social capital (pro-recovery relationships), physical capital (financial stability, housing), human capital (skills, education, health), and cultural capital (values, identity, community belonging). His historical analysis of addiction recovery movements demonstrated that the most successful recovery pathways involve accumulation of diverse capital across multiple domains.

Q1: How does the recovery capital framework fundamentally reframe the clinical problem from 'treating addiction' to 'building recovery'?

It shifts the unit of analysis from the pathology (substance use disorder) to the person-in-context (their total resource landscape).
